Kissinger">Henry Kissinger</a> says there is a new danger, which cannot be addressed by deterrence: "The classical notion of deterrence was that there was some consequences before which aggressors and evildoers would recoil. In a world of suicide bombers, that calculation doesn&#8217;t operate in any comparable way".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/George_Shultz" title="George Shultz">George Shultz</a> has said, "If you think of the people who are doing suicide attacks, and people like that get a nuclear weapon, they are almost by definition not deterrable".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="United_Nations">United Nations</span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Office_for_Disarmament_Affairs" title="United Nations Office for Disarmament Affairs">United Nations Office for Disarmament Affairs</a></div>\n<p>The UN Office for Disarmament Affairs (UNODA) is a department of the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Secretariat" title="United Nations Secretariat">United Nations Secretariat</a> established in January 1998 as part of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/United_Nations_Secretary-General" title="United Nations Secretary-General">United Nations Secretary-General</a> <a href="/wiki/Kofi_Annan" title="Kofi Annan">Kofi Annan</a>\'s plan to reform the UN as presented in his report to the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_General_Assembly" title="United Nations General Assembly">General Assembly</a> in July 1997.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Its goal is to promote nuclear disarmament and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Non-proliferation" title="Non-proliferation">non-proliferation</a> and the strengthening of the disarmament regimes in respect to other weapons of mass destruction, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chemical_weapons" title="Chemical weapons">chemical</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Biological_weapons" title="Biological weapons">biological weapons</a>. It also promotes disarmament efforts in the area of <a href="/wiki/Conventional_weapon" title="Conventional weapon">conventional weapons</a>, especially <a href="/wiki/Land_mine" title="Land mine">land mines</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Small_arms" title="Small arms">small arms</a>, which are often the weapons of choice in contemporary conflicts.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Controversy">Controversy</span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_weapons_debate" title="Nuclear weapons debate">Nuclear weapons debate</a> and <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_anti-nuclear_movement" title="History of the anti-nuclear movement">History of the anti-nuclear movement</a></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ethics">Ethics</span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_ethics" title="Nuclear ethics">Nuclear ethics</a></div>\n<p>Even before the first nuclear weapons had been developed, scientists involved with the <a href="/wiki/Manhattan_Project" title="Manhattan Project">Manhattan Project</a> were divided over the use of the weapon. The role of the two atomic bombings of the country in <a href="/wiki/Surrender_of_Japan" title="Surrender of Japan">Japan\'s surrender</a> and the U.S.\'s <a href="/wiki/Ethics" title="Ethics">ethical</a> justification for them has been the subject of scholarly and popular debate for decades. The question of whether nations should have nuclear weapons, or test them, has been continually and nearly universally controversial.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-brown_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-brown-53">[53]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Notable_nuclear_weapons_accidents">Notable nuclear weapons accidents</span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_and_radiation_accidents_and_incidents" title="Nuclear and radiation accidents and incidents">Nuclear and radiation accidents and incidents</a></div>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_nuclear_close_calls" title="List of nuclear close calls">List of nuclear close calls</a></div>\n<ul><li>21 August 1945: While conducting impromptu experiments on a third core (an alloy of plutonium and gallium) which had been prepared for atomic warfare at <a href="/wiki/Los_Alamos_National_Laboratory" title="Los Alamos National Laboratory">Los Alamos National Laboratory</a>, physicist <a href="/wiki/Harry_Daghlian" title="Harry Daghlian">Harry Daghlian</a> received a lethal dose of radiation. He died on 15 September 1945.</li>\n<li>21 May 1946: While conducting further impromptu experiments on the third plutonium core at Los Alamos National Laboratory, physicist <a href="/wiki/Louis_Slotin" title="Louis Slotin">Louis Slotin</a> received a lethal dose of radiation. He died on 30 May 1946. After these 2 incidents, the core was used to construct a bomb for use on the Nevada Test Range.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup></li>\n<li>February 13, 1950: a <a href="/wiki/1950_British_Columbia_B-36_crash" title="1950 British Columbia B-36 crash">Convair B-36B crashed</a> in northern <a href="/wiki/British_Columbia" title="British Columbia">British Columbia</a> after jettisoning a <a href="/wiki/Mark_4_nuclear_bomb" title="Mark 4 nuclear bomb">Mark IV</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atomic_bomb" title="Atomic bomb">atomic bomb</a>. This was the first such <a href="/wiki/United_States_military_nuclear_incident_terminology" title="United States military nuclear incident terminology">nuclear weapon loss</a> in history.</li>\n<li>May 22, 1957: a 42,000-pound (19,000&#160;kg) <a href="/wiki/Mark_17_nuclear_bomb" title="Mark 17 nuclear bomb">Mark-17 hydrogen bomb</a> accidentally fell from a bomber near Albuquerque, New Mexico. The detonation of the device\'s conventional explosives destroyed it on impact and formed a crater 25 feet (7.6&#160;m) in diameter on land owned by the <a href="/wiki/University_of_New_Mexico" title="University of New Mexico">University of New Mexico</a>. According to a researcher at the Natural Resources Defense Council, it was one of the most powerful bombs made to date.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup></li>\n<li>June 7, 1960: the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/1960_Fort_Dix_IM-99_accident" title="1960 Fort Dix IM-99 accident">1960 Fort Dix IM-99 accident</a> destroyed a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Boeing_CIM-10_Bomarc" title="Boeing CIM-10 Bomarc">Boeing CIM-10 Bomarc</a> nuclear missile and shelter and contaminated the <a href="/wiki/BOMARC_Missile_Accident_Site" title="BOMARC Missile Accident Site">BOMARC Missile Accident Site</a> in New Jersey.</li>\n<li>January 24, 1961: the <a href="/wiki/1961_Goldsboro_B-52_crash" title="1961 Goldsboro B-52 crash">1961 Goldsboro B-52 crash</a> occurred near <a href="/wiki/Goldsboro,_North_Carolina" title="Goldsboro, North Carolina">Goldsboro, North Carolina</a>. A <a href="/wiki/Boeing_B-52_Stratofortress" title="Boeing B-52 Stratofortress">Boeing B-52 Stratofortress</a> carrying two <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/W39" title="W39">Mark 39</a> nuclear bombs broke up in mid-air, dropping its nuclear payload in the process.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BOAS_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-BOAS-56">[56]</a></sup></li>\n<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/1965_Philippine_Sea_A-4_crash" title="1965 Philippine Sea A-4 crash">1965 Philippine Sea A-4 crash</a>, where a <a href="/wiki/Douglas_A-4_Skyhawk" title="Douglas A-4 Skyhawk">Skyhawk</a> attack aircraft with a nuclear weapon fell into the sea.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CruiseReport_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-CruiseReport-57">[57]</a></sup> The pilot, the aircraft, and the <a href="/wiki/B43_nuclear_bomb" title="B43 nuclear bomb">B43 nuclear bomb</a> were never recovered.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> It was not until 1989 that <a href="/wiki/The_Pentagon" title="The Pentagon">the Pentagon</a> revealed the loss of the one-megaton bomb.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup></li>\n<li>January 17, 1966: the <a href="/wiki/1966_Palomares_B-52_crash" title="1966 Palomares B-52 crash">1966 Palomares B-52 crash</a> occurred when a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/B-52_Stratofortress" title="B-52 Stratofortress">B-52G bomber</a> of the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Air_Force" title="United States Air Force">USAF</a> collided with a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/KC-135_Stratotanker" title="KC-135 Stratotanker">KC-135 tanker</a> during <a href="/wiki/Aerial_refueling" title="Aerial refueling">mid-air refuelling</a> off the coast of <a href="/wiki/Spain" title="Spain">Spain</a>. The KC-135 was completely destroyed when its fuel load ignited, killing all four crew members. The B-52G broke apart, killing three of the seven crew members aboard.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hayes_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-hayes-60">[60]</a></sup> Of the four <a href="/wiki/B28_nuclear_bomb" title="B28 nuclear bomb">Mk28</a> type <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Teller%E2%80%93Ulam_design" title="Teller&#8211;Ulam design">hydrogen bombs</a> the B-52G carried,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> three were found on land near <a href="/wiki/Almer%C3%ADa" title="Almer&#237;a">Almer&#237;a</a>, Spain. The non-nuclear explosives in two of the weapons detonated upon impact with the ground, resulting in the contamination of a 2-square-kilometer (490-acre) (0.78 square mile) area by <a href="/wiki/Radioactive_decay" title="Radioactive decay">radioactive</a> <a href="/wiki/Plutonium" title="Plutonium">plutonium</a>. The fourth, which fell into the <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_Sea" title="Mediterranean Sea">Mediterranean Sea</a>, was recovered intact after a 2&#189;-month-long search.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-long_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-long-62">[62]</a></sup></li>\n<li>January 21, 1968: the <a href="/wiki/1968_Thule_Air_Base_B-52_crash" title="1968 Thule Air Base B-52 crash">1968 Thule Air Base B-52 crash</a> involved a <a href="/wiki/United_States_Air_Force" title="United States Air Force">United States Air Force</a> (USAF) <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/B-52_Stratofortress" title="B-52 Stratofortress">B-52 bomber</a>. The aircraft was carrying four <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydrogen_bomb" title="Hydrogen bomb">hydrogen bombs</a> when a cabin fire forced the crew to abandon the aircraft. Six crew members ejected safely, but one who did not have an <a href="/wiki/Ejection_seat" title="Ejection seat">ejection seat</a> was killed while trying to bail out. The bomber crashed onto <a href="/wiki/Sea_ice" title="Sea ice">sea ice</a> in <a href="/wiki/Greenland" title="Greenland">Greenland</a>, causing the nuclear payload to rupture and disperse, which resulted in widespread <a href="/wiki/Radioactive_contamination" title="Radioactive contamination">radioactive contamination</a>.</li>\n<li>September 18&#8211;19, 1980: the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/1980_Damascus,_Arkansas_incident" title="1980 Damascus, Arkansas incident">Damascus Accident</a>, occurred in Damascus, Arkansas, where a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Titan_missile" title="Titan missile">Titan missile</a> equipped with a nuclear warhead exploded. The accident was caused by a maintenance man who dropped a socket from a socket wrench down an 80-foot (24&#160;m) shaft, puncturing a fuel tank on the rocket. Leaking fuel resulted in a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hypergolic" title="Hypergolic">hypergolic</a> fuel explosion, jettisoning the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/W-53_warhead" title="W-53 warhead">W-53 warhead</a> beyond the launch site.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup></li></ul>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nuclear_testing_and_fallout">Nuclear testing and fallout</span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fallout" title="Nuclear fallout">Nuclear fallout</a></div> <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Downwinders" title="Downwinders">Downwinders</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:502px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Rael_Nuclear_use_locations_world_map.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="625" data-file-width="1425" decoding="async" height="219" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Rael_Nuclear_use_locations_world_map.png/500px-Rael_Nuclear_use_locations_world_map.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Rael_Nuclear_use_locations_world_map.png/750px-Rael_Nuclear_use_locations_world_map.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Rael_Nuclear_use_locations_world_map.png/1000px-Rael_Nuclear_use_locations_world_map.png 2x" width="500"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Rael_Nuclear_use_locations_world_map.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Over 2,000 nuclear tests have been conducted in over a dozen different sites around the world. Red Russia/Soviet Union, blue France, light blue United States, violet Britain, black Israel, yellow China, orange India, brown Pakistan, green North Korea and light green (territories exposed to nuclear bombs)</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:NNSA-NSO-787.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="284" data-file-width="360" decoding="async" height="174" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/NNSA-NSO-787.jpg/220px-NNSA-NSO-787.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/NNSA-NSO-787.jpg/330px-NNSA-NSO-787.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/e/eb/NNSA-NSO-787.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:NNSA-NSO-787.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>This view of downtown <a href="/wiki/Las_Vegas" title="Las Vegas">Las Vegas</a> shows a <a href="/wiki/Mushroom_cloud" title="Mushroom cloud">mushroom cloud</a> in the background. Scenes such as this were typical during the 1950s. From 1951 to 1962 the government conducted 100 atmospheric tests at the nearby <a href="/wiki/Nevada_Test_Site" title="Nevada Test Site">Nevada Test Site</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p>Over 500 atmospheric nuclear weapons tests were conducted at various sites around the world from 1945 to 1980. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radioactive_fallout" title="Radioactive fallout">Radioactive fallout</a> from nuclear weapons testing was first drawn to public attention in 1954 when the <a href="/wiki/Castle_Bravo" title="Castle Bravo">Castle Bravo</a> hydrogen bomb test at the <a href="/wiki/Pacific_Proving_Grounds" title="Pacific Proving Grounds">Pacific Proving Grounds</a> contaminated the crew and catch of the Japanese fishing boat <i><a href="/wiki/Daigo_Fukury%C5%AB_Maru" title="Daigo Fukury&#363; Maru">Lucky Dragon</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rudig2_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-rudig2-66">[66]</a></sup> One of the fishermen died in Japan seven months later, and the fear of contaminated <a href="/wiki/Tuna" title="Tuna">tuna</a> led to a temporary boycotting of the popular staple in Japan. The incident caused widespread concern around the world, especially regarding the effects of <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fallout" title="Nuclear fallout">nuclear fallout</a> and atmospheric <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nuclear_testing" title="Nuclear testing">nuclear testing</a>, and "provided a decisive impetus for the emergence of the anti-nuclear weapons movement in many countries".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rudig2_66-1"><a href="#cite_note-rudig2-66">[66]</a></sup>\n</p><p>As public awareness and concern mounted over the possible health hazards associated with exposure to the <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fallout" title="Nuclear fallout">nuclear fallout</a>, various studies were done to assess the extent of the hazard. A <a href="/wiki/Centers_for_Disease_Control_and_Prevention" title="Centers for Disease Control and Prevention">Centers for Disease Control and Prevention</a>/ <a href="/wiki/National_Cancer_Institute" title="National Cancer Institute">National Cancer Institute</a> study claims that fallout from atmospheric nuclear tests would lead to perhaps 11,000 excess deaths among people alive during atmospheric testing in the United States from all forms of cancer, including leukemia, from 1951 to well into the 21st century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup>\nAs of  March&#160;2009<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Nuclear_weapon&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, the U.S. is the only nation that compensates nuclear test victims. Since the <a href="/wiki/Radiation_Exposure_Compensation_Act" title="Radiation Exposure Compensation Act">Radiation Exposure Compensation Act</a> of 1990, more than $1.38 billion in compensation has been approved. The money is going to people who took part in the tests, notably at the <a href="/wiki/Nevada_Test_Site" title="Nevada Test Site">Nevada Test Site</a>, and to others exposed to the radiation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-compo_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-compo-69">[69]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In addition, leakage of byproducts of nuclear weapon production into groundwater has been an ongoing issue, particularly at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hanford_site" title="Hanford site">Hanford site</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hanford_Ref_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hanford_Ref-71">[71]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Effects_of_nuclear_explosions">Effects of nuclear explosions</span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Effects_of_nuclear_explosions" title="Effects of nuclear explosions">Effects of nuclear explosions</a></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Effects_of_nuclear_explosions_on_human_health">Effects of nuclear explosions on human health</span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Effects_of_nuclear_explosions_on_human_health" title="Effects of nuclear explosions on human health">Effects of nuclear explosions on human health</a></div>\n<p>Some scientists estimate that a nuclear war with 100 Hiroshima-size nuclear explosions on cities could cost the lives of tens of millions of people from long term climatic effects alone. The climatology hypothesis is that <i>if</i> each city <a href="/wiki/Firestorm" title="Firestorm">firestorms</a>, a great deal of soot could be thrown up into the atmosphere which could blanket the earth, cutting out sunlight for years on end, causing the disruption of food chains, in what is termed a <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_winter" title="Nuclear winter">nuclear winter</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup>\n</p><p>People near the Hiroshima explosion and who managed to survive the explosion subsequently suffered a variety of medical effects:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-remm.nlm.gov_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-remm.nlm.gov-74">[74]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="better sources needed here (July 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<ul><li>Initial stage&#8212;the first 1&#8211;9 weeks, in which are the greatest number of deaths, with 90% due to thermal injury and/or blast effects and 10% due to super-lethal <a href="/wiki/Radiation" title="Radiation">radiation</a> exposure.</li>\n<li>Intermediate stage&#8212;from 10&#8211;12 weeks. The deaths in this period are from <a href="/wiki/Ionizing_radiation" title="Ionizing radiation">ionizing radiation</a> in the median lethal range &#8211; <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/LD50" title="LD50">LD50</a></li>\n<li>Late period&#8212;lasting from 13&#8211;20 weeks. This period has some improvement in survivors\' condition.</li>\n<li>Delayed period&#8212;from 20+ weeks. Characterized by numerous complications, mostly related to healing of thermal and mechanical injuries, and if the individual was exposed to a few hundred to a thousand <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Millisievert" title="Millisievert">millisieverts</a> of radiation, it is coupled with infertility, sub-fertility and blood disorders. Furthermore, ionizing radiation above a dose of around 50&#8211;100 millisievert exposure has been shown to statistically begin increasing one\'s chance of dying of cancer sometime in their lifetime over the normal unexposed rate of ~25%, in the long term, a heightened rate of cancer, proportional to the dose received, would begin to be observed after ~5+ years, with lesser problems such as eye <a href="/wiki/Cataract" title="Cataract">cataracts</a> and other more minor effects in other organs and tissue also being observed over the long term.</li></ul>\n<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fallout" title="Fallout">Fallout</a> exposure &#8211; Depending on if further afield individuals <a href="/wiki/Shelter_in_place" title="Shelter in place">shelter in place</a> or evacuate perpendicular to the direction of the wind, and therefore avoid contact with the fallout plume, and stay there for the days and weeks after the nuclear explosion, their exposure to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fallout" title="Fallout">fallout</a>, and therefore their total dose, will vary. With those who do shelter in place, and or evacuate, experiencing a total dose that would be negligible in comparison to someone who just went about their life as normal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Staying indoors until after the most hazardous fallout <a href="/wiki/Isotope" title="Isotope">isotope</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/I-131" title="I-131">I-131</a> decays away to 0.1% of its initial quantity after ten <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Half_life" title="Half life">half lifes</a> &#8211; which is represented by 80 days in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/I-131" title="I-131">I-131s</a> case, would make the difference between likely contracting <a href="/wiki/Thyroid_cancer" title="Thyroid cancer">Thyroid cancer</a> or escaping completely from this substance depending on the actions of the individual.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Public_opposition">Public opposition</span></h4>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_anti-nuclear_movement" title="History of the anti-nuclear movement">History of the anti-nuclear movement</a> and <a href="/wiki/International_Day_against_Nuclear_Tests" title="International Day against Nuclear Tests">International Day against Nuclear Tests</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Women_Strike_for_Peace_NYWTS.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3584" data-file-width="2903" decoding="async" height="210" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Women_Strike_for_Peace_NYWTS.jpg/170px-Women_Strike_for_Peace_NYWTS.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Women_Strike_for_Peace_NYWTS.jpg/255px-Women_Strike_for_Peace_NYWTS.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Women_Strike_for_Peace_NYWTS.jpg/340px-Women_Strike_for_Peace_NYWTS.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Women_Strike_for_Peace_NYWTS.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Women_Strike_for_Peace" title="Women Strike for Peace">Women Strike for Peace</a> during the <a href="/wiki/Cuban_Missile_Crisis" title="Cuban Missile Crisis">Cuban Missile Crisis</a></div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Essais_nucleaires_manif.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1695" data-file-width="3486" decoding="async" height="107" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Essais_nucleaires_manif.jpg/220px-Essais_nucleaires_manif.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Essais_nucleaires_manif.jpg/330px-Essais_nucleaires_manif.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Essais_nucleaires_manif.jpg/440px-Essais_nucleaires_manif.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Essais_nucleaires_manif.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Demonstration against nuclear testing in <a href="/wiki/Lyon" title="Lyon">Lyon</a>, France, in the 1980s.</div></div></div>\n<p>Peace movements emerged in Japan and in 1954 they converged to form a unified "Japanese Council Against Atomic and Hydrogen Bombs". Japanese opposition to nuclear weapons tests in the Pacific Ocean was widespread, and "an estimated 35 million signatures were collected on petitions calling for bans on nuclear weapons".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-falkj_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-falkj-80">[80]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In the United Kingdom, the first <a href="/wiki/Aldermaston_Marches" title="Aldermaston Marches">Aldermaston March</a> organised by the <a href="/wiki/Campaign_for_Nuclear_Disarmament" title="Campaign for Nuclear Disarmament">Campaign for Nuclear Disarmament</a>(CND) took place at <a href="/wiki/Easter" title="Easter">Easter</a> 1958, when, according to the CND, several thousand people marched for four days from <a href="/wiki/Trafalgar_Square" title="Trafalgar Square">Trafalgar Square</a>, London, to the <a href="/wiki/Atomic_Weapons_Establishment" title="Atomic Weapons Establishment">Atomic Weapons Research Establishment</a> close to <a href="/wiki/Aldermaston" title="Aldermaston">Aldermaston</a> in <a href="/wiki/Berkshire" title="Berkshire">Berkshire</a>, England, to demonstrate their opposition to nuclear weapons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CND_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-CND-81">[81]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GuardianUnlimited:1958_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-GuardianUnlimited:1958-82">[82]</a></sup> The Aldermaston marches continued into the late 1960s when tens of thousands of people took part in the four-day marches.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-falkj_80-1"><a href="#cite_note-falkj-80">[80]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1959, a letter in the <i>Bulletin of the Atomic Scientists</i> was the start of a successful campaign to stop the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Atomic_Energy_Commission" title="United States Atomic Energy Commission">Atomic Energy Commission</a> dumping <a href="/wiki/Radioactive_waste" title="Radioactive waste">radioactive waste</a><sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Dates_and_numbers" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Dates and numbers"><span title="This term requires quantification. (May 2013)">quantify</span></a></i>]</sup> in the sea 19 kilometres from <a href="/wiki/Boston" title="Boston">Boston</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup> In 1962, <a href="/wiki/Linus_Pauling" title="Linus Pauling">Linus Pauling</a> won the <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Peace_Prize" title="Nobel Peace Prize">Nobel Peace Prize</a> for his work to stop the atmospheric testing of nuclear weapons, and the "Ban the Bomb" movement spread.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-brown_53-1"><a href="#cite_note-brown-53">[53]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1963, many countries ratified the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Partial_Test_Ban_Treaty" title="Partial Test Ban Treaty">Partial Test Ban Treaty</a> prohibiting atmospheric nuclear testing. Radioactive fallout became less of an issue and the anti-nuclear weapons movement went into decline for some years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rudig2_66-2"><a href="#cite_note-rudig2-66">[66]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup> A resurgence of interest occurred amid European and American <a href="/wiki/Radiophobia" title="Radiophobia">fears of nuclear war</a> in the 1980s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Costs_and_technology_spin-offs">Costs and technology spin-offs</span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Global_Positioning_System" title="Global Positioning System">Global Positioning System</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_weapons_delivery" title="Nuclear weapons delivery">Nuclear weapons delivery</a>, <a href="/wiki/History_of_computing_hardware" title="History of computing hardware">History of computing hardware</a>, <a href="/wiki/ENIAC" title="ENIAC">ENIAC</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Swords_to_ploughshares" title="Swords to ploughshares">Swords to ploughshares</a></div>\n<p>According to an audit by the <a href="/wiki/Brookings_Institution" title="Brookings Institution">Brookings Institution</a>, between 1940 and 1996, the U.S. spent $9.3&#160;trillion in present-day terms<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-inflation-US_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-inflation-US-86">[86]</a></sup> on nuclear weapons programs. 57 percent of which was spent on building <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_weapons_delivery" title="Nuclear weapons delivery">nuclear weapons delivery</a> systems. 6.3 percent of the total, $583&#160;billion in present-day terms, was spent on <a href="/wiki/Environmental_remediation" title="Environmental remediation">environmental remediation</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nuclear_waste_management" title="Nuclear waste management">nuclear waste management</a>, for example cleaning up the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hanford_site" title="Hanford site">Hanford site</a>, and 7 percent of the total, $653&#160;billion was spent on making nuclear weapons themselves.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Non-weapons_uses">Non-weapons uses</span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Peaceful_nuclear_explosion" title="Peaceful nuclear explosion">Peaceful nuclear explosion</a></div>\n<p>Peaceful nuclear explosions are <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_explosion" title="Nuclear explosion">nuclear explosions</a> conducted for non-military purposes, such as activities related to <a href="/wiki/Economic_development" title="Economic development">economic development</a> including the creation of <a href="/wiki/Canal" title="Canal">canals</a>. During the 1960s and 1970s, both the United States and the Soviet Union conducted a number of PNEs. Six of the explosions by the Soviet Union are considered to have been of an applied nature, not just tests.\n</p><p>Subsequently, the United States and the Soviet Union halted their programs. Definitions and limits are covered in the Peaceful Nuclear Explosions Treaty of 1976.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Comprehensive_Nuclear-Test-Ban_Treaty" title="Comprehensive Nuclear-Test-Ban Treaty">Comprehensive Nuclear-Test-Ban Treaty</a> of 1996, once it enters into force, will prohibit all nuclear explosions, regardless of whether they are for peaceful purposes or not.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History_of_development">History of development</span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_nuclear_weapons" title="History of nuclear weapons">History of nuclear weapons</a></div>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Soviet_atomic_bomb_project" title="Soviet atomic bomb project">Soviet atomic bomb project</a>, <a href="/wiki/Manhattan_Project" title="Manhattan Project">Manhattan Project</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cold_War" title="Cold War">Cold War</a>, and <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Teller%E2%80%93Ulam_design" title="History of the Teller&#8211;Ulam design">History of the Teller&#8211;Ulam design</a></div>\n<table class="box-Empty_section plainlinks metadata ambox mbox-small-left ambox-content" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><a class="image" LXV, no. 1 (18 January 2018), pp.&#160;13&#8211;15.</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Eric_Schlosser" title="Eric Schlosser">Eric Schlosser</a>, <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Command_and_Control:_Nuclear_Weapons,_the_Damascus_Accident,_and_the_Illusion_of_Safety" title="Command and Control: Nuclear Weapons, the Damascus Accident, and the Illusion of Safety">Command and Control: Nuclear Weapons, the Damascus Accident, and the Illusion of Safety</a></i>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Penguin_Press" title="Penguin Press">Penguin Press</a>, 2013, <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r879151008" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/1594202273" title="Special:BookSources/1594202273">1594202273</a>. The book became the basis for a 2-hour 2017 <a href="/wiki/PBS" title="PBS">PBS</a> <a href="/wiki/American_Experience" title="American Experience">American Experience</a> episode, likewise titled "Command and Control". Nuclear weapons continue to be equally hazardous to their owners as to their potential targets. Under the 1970 <a href="/wiki/Treaty_on_the_Non-Proliferation_of_Nuclear_Weapons" title="Treaty on the Non-Proliferation of Nuclear Weapons">Treaty on the Non-Proliferation of Nuclear Weapons</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nuclear-weapon_states" title="Nuclear-weapon states">nuclear-weapon states</a> are obliged to work toward the elimination of nuclear weapons.</li></ul>\n<h2><span
